					<h1 id="section_0">Juniper Networks</h1>

			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"JNPR" redirects here. For the characters from <i>RWBY</i>, see <a href="/wiki/Team_JNPR" class="mw-redirect" title="Team JNPR">Team JNPR</a>.</div>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<p><b>Juniper Networks, Inc.</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporation</a> headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Sunnyvale,_California" title="Sunnyvale, California">Sunnyvale, California</a>. The company develops and markets networking products, including <a href="/wiki/Router_(computing)" title="Router (computing)">routers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Network_switch" title="Network switch">switches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Network_management" title="Network management">network management</a> software, <a href="/wiki/Network_security" title="Network security">network security</a> products, and <a href="/wiki/Software-defined_networking" title="Software-defined networking">software-defined networking</a> technology.

style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://juniper.net">juniper<wbr></wbr>.net</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table><p>The company was founded in 1996 by <a href="/wiki/Pradeep_Sindhu" title="Pradeep Sindhu">Pradeep Sindhu</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Scott_Kriens" title="Scott Kriens">Scott Kriens</a> as the first CEO, who remained until September 2008. Kriens has been credited with much of Juniper's early market success.<sup id="cite_ref-forbestopexec_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbestopexec-4">[4]</a></sup> It received several rounds of funding from <a href="/wiki/Venture_capital" title="Venture capital">venture capitalists</a> and telecommunications companies before <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">going public</a> in 1999. Juniper grew to $673 million in annual revenues by 2000. By 2001 it had a 37% share of the core routers market, challenging <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems" title="Cisco Systems">Cisco</a>'s once-dominant market-share.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aoiuhfedo_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aoiuhfedo-6">[6]</a></sup> It grew to $4 billion in revenues by 2004 and $4.63 billion in 2014. Juniper appointed <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Johnson_(executive)" title="Kevin Johnson (executive)">Kevin Johnson</a> as CEO in 2008, <a href="/wiki/Shaygan_Kheradpir" title="Shaygan Kheradpir">Shaygan Kheradpir</a> in 2013 and Rami Rahim in 2014.
</p><p>Juniper Networks originally focused on <a href="/wiki/Core_router" title="Core router">core routers</a>, which are used by <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">internet service providers</a> (ISPs) to perform <a href="/wiki/Whois" class="mw-redirect" title="Whois">IP address lookups</a> and direct <a href="/wiki/Internet_traffic" title="Internet traffic">internet traffic</a>. Through the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Unisphere_Networks" title="Unisphere Networks">Unisphere</a>, in 2002, the company entered the market for <a href="/wiki/Router_(computing)" title="Router (computing)">edge routers</a>, which are used by ISPs to route internet traffic to individual consumers. In 2003, Juniper entered the <a href="/wiki/IT_security" class="mw-redirect" title="IT security">IT security</a> market with its own JProtect security toolkit before <a href="/wiki/Takeover" title="Takeover">acquiring</a> security company <a href="/wiki/NetScreen_Technologies" title="NetScreen Technologies">NetScreen Technologies</a> the following year. In the early 2000s, Juniper entered the enterprise segment, which accounted for one-third of its revenues by 2005. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Juniper has been focused on developing new <a href="/wiki/Software-defined_networking" title="Software-defined networking">software-defined networking</a> products.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none"><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_funding">Origins and funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins and funding" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Juniper_Networks_found_Pradeep_Sindhu_headshot.jpeg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Juniper_Networks_found_Pradeep_Sindhu_headshot.jpeg/175px-Juniper_Networks_found_Pradeep_Sindhu_headshot.jpeg" decoding="async" width="175" height="245" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="504"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 175px;height: 245px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Juniper_Networks_found_Pradeep_Sindhu_headshot.jpeg/175px-Juniper_Networks_found_Pradeep_Sindhu_headshot.jpeg" data-alt="" data-width="175" data-height="245" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Juniper_Networks_found_Pradeep_Sindhu_headshot.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juniper founder Pradeep Sindhu</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pradeep_Sindhu" title="Pradeep Sindhu">Pradeep Sindhu</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-economist_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-7">[7]</a></sup> a scientist with <a href="/wiki/Xerox" title="Xerox">Xerox</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto_Research_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Palo Alto Research Center">Palo Alto Research Center</a> (PARC),<sup id="cite_ref-five_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-five-8">[8]</a></sup> conceived the idea for Juniper Networks while on vacation in 1995<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup>
and founded the company in February 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-dateoffoundation_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dateoffoundation-10">[10]</a></sup> At the time, most routers used for Internet traffic were intended for phone calls and had dedicated circuits for each caller (<a href="/wiki/Circuit_switching" title="Circuit switching">circuit switching</a>). Sindhu wanted to create <a href="/wiki/Data_packet" class="mw-redirect" title="Data packet">data packet</a>-based routers that were optimized for Internet traffic (<a href="/wiki/Packet_switching" title="Packet switching">packet switching</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-twelve_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twelve-11">[11]</a></sup> whereby the <a href="/wiki/Routing" title="Routing">routing</a> and transferring of data occurs "by means of addressed packets so that a <a href="/wiki/Communication_channel" title="Communication channel">channel</a> is occupied during the <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(telecommunications)" title="Transmission (telecommunications)">transmission</a> of the packet only, and upon completion of the transmission the channel is made available for the transfer of other <a href="/wiki/Network_traffic" title="Network traffic">traffic</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> He was joined by engineers Bjorn Liencres from <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">Sun Microsystems</a> and Dennis Ferguson from <a href="/wiki/MCI_Communications" title="MCI Communications">MCI Communications</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p><p>Sindhu started Juniper Networks with $2 million in <a href="/wiki/Seed_money" title="Seed money">seed funding</a>, which was followed by $12 million in funding in the company's first year of operations.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> About seven months after the company's founding, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Kriens" title="Scott Kriens">Scott Kriens</a> was appointed CEO to manage the business, while founder Sindhu became the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Technology_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Technology Officer">Chief Technology Officer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-five_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-five-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-six_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-six-16">[16]</a></sup> By February 1997, Juniper had raised $8 million in <a href="/wiki/Venture_capital" title="Venture capital">venture funding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Later that year, Juniper Networks raised an additional $40 million in investments<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> from a round that included four out of five of the largest telecommunications equipment manufacturers: <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nortel" title="Nortel">Nortel</a> and <a href="/wiki/3Com" title="3Com">3Com</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eight_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eight-22">[22]</a></sup> Juniper also received $2.5 million from <a href="/wiki/Qwest" title="Qwest">Qwest</a> and other investments from <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_and_IPO">Growth and IPO</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Growth and IPO" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Juniper Networks had $3.8 million in annual revenue in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-three_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-three-24">[24]</a></sup> By the following year, its only product, the M40 router, was being used by 50 telecommunications companies.<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup> Juniper Networks signed agreements with <a href="/wiki/Alcatel-Lucent" title="Alcatel-Lucent">Alcatel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a> to distribute the M40 internationally. A European headquarters was established in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and an Asia-Pacific headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>. A subsidiary was created in Japan<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup> and offices were established in Korea in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Juniper Networks's market share for <a href="/wiki/Core_router" title="Core router">core routers</a> grew from 6% in 1998 to 17.5% one year later,<sup id="cite_ref-five_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-five-8">[8]</a></sup> and 20% by April 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p><p>Juniper Networks filed for an <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> in April 1999<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and its first day on the <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" class="mw-redirect" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a> was that June.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The stock set a record in first-day trading in the technology sector by increasing 191%<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> to a market capitalization of $4.9 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> According to <i>Telephony</i>, Juniper Networks became the "latest darling of Wall Street",<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-street_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-street-32">[32]</a></sup> reaching a $7 billion valuation by late July.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Within a year, the company's stock grew five-fold.<sup id="cite_ref-five_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-five-8">[8]</a></sup></p><p>Juniper Networks's revenues grew 600% in 2000 to $673 million.<sup id="cite_ref-two_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-34">[34]</a></sup> That same year, Juniper Networks moved its headquarters from <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sunnyvale,_California" title="Sunnyvale, California">Sunnyvale, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition">Competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Competition" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>By 2001, Juniper controlled one-third of the market for high-end core routers, mostly at the expense of <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems" title="Cisco Systems">Cisco Systems</a> sales.<sup id="cite_ref-three_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-three-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-street_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-street-32">[32]</a></sup> According to <i>BusinessWeek</i>, "analysts unanimously agree[d] that Juniper's boxes [were] technically superior to Cisco's because the hardware does most of the data processing. Cisco routers still relied on software, which often results in slower speeds."<sup id="cite_ref-two_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-34">[34]</a></sup> However, Cisco provided a broader range of services and support and had an entrenched market position.<sup id="cite_ref-five_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-five-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-two_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-34">[34]</a></sup> The press often depicted Juniper and Cisco as a "<a href="/wiki/Goliath" title="Goliath">David versus Goliath</a>" story.<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-twelve_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twelve-11">[11]</a></sup> Cisco had grown through acquisitions to be a large generalist vendor for routing equipment in homes, businesses and for ISPs, whereas Juniper was thought of as the "anti-Cisco" for being a small company with a narrow focus.<sup id="cite_ref-economist_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-two_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fifteen_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fifteen-35">[35]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2001, Cisco introduced a suite of router products that <i>BusinessWeek</i> said was intended to challenge Juniper's increasing market-share.<sup id="cite_ref-two_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-34">[34]</a></sup> According to <i>BusinessWeek</i>, Juniper's top-end router was four times as fast at only twice the cost of comparable Cisco products.<sup id="cite_ref-seven_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seven-36">[36]</a></sup> Cisco's routers were not expected to erode Juniper's growing share of the market, but other companies such as <a href="/wiki/Lucent" title="Lucent">Lucent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcatel-Lucent" title="Alcatel-Lucent">Alcatel</a>, and startups Avici Systems and Pluris had announced plans to release products that would out-pace Juniper's routers.<sup id="cite_ref-two_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-34">[34]</a></sup></p><p>Juniper introduced a suite of routers for the network edge that allowed it to compete with Cisco. Juniper's edge routers had a 9% market share two months after release.<sup id="cite_ref-three_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-three-24">[24]</a></sup> Both companies made exaggerated marketing claims; Juniper promoted its products as stable enough to make IT staff bored and Cisco announced lab tests from <i><a href="/wiki/Light_Reading" title="Light Reading">Light Reading</a></i> proved its products were superior to Juniper, whereas the publication itself reached the opposite conclusion.<sup id="cite_ref-three_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-three-24">[24]</a></sup> By 2002, both companies were repeatedly announcing products with faster specifications than the other in what <i>Network World</i> called a "'speeds-and-feeds' public relations contest".<sup id="cite_ref-aoiuhfedo_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aoiuhfedo-6">[6]</a></sup></p><p>By 2004, Juniper controlled 38% of the core router market.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> By 2007, it had a 5%, 18% and 30% share of the market for enterprise, edge and core routers respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alcatel-Lucent" title="Alcatel-Lucent">Alcatel-Lucent</a> was unsuccessful in challenging Juniper in the core router market but continued competing with Juniper in edge routers along with Cisco.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_development">Further development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Further development" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In late 2000, Juniper formed a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a> to develop and market <a href="/wiki/Network_switch" title="Network switch">network switches</a> for internet traffic on mobile devices,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and with <a href="/wiki/Nortel" title="Nortel">Nortel</a> for <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">fiber optic</a> technology.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 2001, Juniper introduced a technical certification program and was involved in the first optical internet network in China.<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pavlichek2002_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlichek2002-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>12</span></sup> Juniper's growth slowed in 2001 as the telecommunications sector experienced a slowdown<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and revenues fell by two-thirds during the <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">dot-com</a> bust.<sup id="cite_ref-businesstwo_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businesstwo-46">[46]</a></sup> 9 to 10% of its workforce was laid off.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p><p>Juniper had rebounded by 2004, surpassing $1 billion in revenues for the first time that year<sup id="cite_ref-businesstwo_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businesstwo-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-primaryone_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primaryone-48">[48]</a></sup> and reaching $2 billion in revenue in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Beginning in 2004, with the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/NetScreen_Technologies" title="NetScreen Technologies">NetScreen</a>, Juniper Networks began developing and marketing products for the enterprise segment.<sup id="cite_ref-corporate_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corporate-50">[50]</a></sup> Juniper had a reputation for serving ISPs, not enterprises, which it was trying to change.<sup id="cite_ref-appleomega_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appleomega-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dfaiuh_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dfaiuh-52">[52]</a></sup> By 2005 enterprise customers accounted for one-third of the company's revenues,<sup id="cite_ref-corporate_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corporate-50">[50]</a></sup> but it had spent $5 billion in acquisitions and <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">R&amp;D</a> for the enterprise market.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p><p>In 2006, more than 200 US companies restated their financial results due to a series of investigations into <a href="/wiki/Options_backdating" title="Options backdating">stock backdating</a> practices.<sup id="cite_ref-bloom_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloom-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Juniper stockholders alleged the company engaged in deceptive backdating practices that benefited its top executives unfairly.<sup id="cite_ref-bloom_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloom-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reutersss_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersss-56">[56]</a></sup> In December 2006, Juniper restated its financials, charging $900 million in expenses to correct backdated stock options from 1999 to 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-bloom_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloom-54">[54]</a></sup> This was followed by a $169 million settlement with stockholders in February 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-reutersss_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersss-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2008.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2008–present">2008–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2008–present" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In July 2008 Juniper's first CEO, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Kriens" title="Scott Kriens">Scott Kriens</a>, became chairman and former <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> executive <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Johnson_(executive)" title="Kevin Johnson (executive)">Kevin Johnson</a> was appointed CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Johnson focused the company more on software, creating a software solutions division headed by a former Microsoft colleague, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Muglia" title="Bob Muglia">Bob Muglia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cross_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cross-59">[59]</a></sup> Juniper also hired other former Microsoft executives to focus on the company's software strategy and encourage developers to create software products that run on the <a href="/wiki/Junos" class="mw-redirect" title="Junos">Junos operating system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Juniper established partnerships with <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, Microsoft and <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Corporation" title="Oracle Corporation">Oracle</a> for software compatibility efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The SSL/VPN Pulse product family was launched in 2010, then later spun off to a private equity firm in 2014 for $250 million.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p><p>In 2012, Juniper laid off 5% of its staff and four of its high-ranking executives departed.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The following year, CEO <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Johnson_(executive)" title="Kevin Johnson (executive)">Kevin Johnson</a> announced he was retiring once a replacement was found.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In November 2013, Juniper Networks announced that <a href="/wiki/Shaygan_Kheradpir" title="Shaygan Kheradpir">Shaygan Kheradpir</a> would be appointed as the new CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> He started the position in January 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2014, <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a>, activist investor and Juniper shareholder <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Associates" class="mw-redirect" title="Elliott Associates">Elliott Associates</a> advocated that Juniper reduce its cash reserves and cut costs,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> before Kheradpir was officially appointed.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> That February, Juniper reached an agreement with Elliott and other stakeholders for an Integrated Operating Plan (IOP) that involved repurchasing $2 billion in shares, reducing operating expenses by $160 million and appointing two new directors to its board.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> That April, 6% of the company's staff were laid off to cut expenses.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In November 2014, Kheradpir unexpectedly resigned following a review by Juniper's board of directors regarding his conduct in a negotiation with an unnamed Juniper customer.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> An internal Juniper executive, Rami Rahim, took his place as CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2014, <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto_Networks" title="Palo Alto Networks">Palo Alto Networks</a> agreed to pay a $175 million settlement for allegedly infringing on Juniper's patents for application firewalls.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p><p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired Magazine</a> reported that the company announced it had found unauthorized code that enabled <a href="/wiki/Backdoor_(computing)" title="Backdoor (computing)">backdoors</a> into its <a href="/wiki/ScreenOS" title="ScreenOS">ScreenOS</a> products.<sup id="cite_ref-wired_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired-77">[77]</a></sup> The code was patched with updates from the company.<sup id="cite_ref-wired_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisitions_and_investments">Acquisitions and investments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Acquisitions and investments" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_acquisitions_by_Juniper_Networks" title="List of acquisitions by Juniper Networks">List of acquisitions by Juniper Networks</a></div>
<p>By 2001, Juniper had made only a few acquisitions of smaller companies, due to the leadership's preference for organic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-fifteen_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fifteen-35">[35]</a></sup> The pace of acquisition picked up in 2001 and 2002 with the purchases of <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Broadband_Communications" title="Pacific Broadband Communications">Pacific Broadband</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unisphere_Networks" title="Unisphere Networks">Unisphere Networks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-multichannel_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-multichannel-78">[78]</a></sup> In 2004 Juniper made a $4 billion acquisition of network security company <a href="/wiki/NetScreen_Technologies" title="NetScreen Technologies">NetScreen Technologies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Juniper revised NetScreen's channel program that year and used its reseller network to bring other products to market.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p><p>Juniper made five acquisitions in 2005, mostly of startups with deal values ranging from $8.7 to $337 million. It acquired application-acceleration vendor <a href="/wiki/Redline_Networks" title="Redline Networks">Redline Networks</a>, VOIP company <a href="/wiki/Kagoor_Networks" title="Kagoor Networks">Kagoor Networks</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Wide_area_network" title="Wide area network">wide area network</a> (WAN) company <a href="/wiki/Peribit_Networks" title="Peribit Networks">Peribit Networks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Peribit and Redline were incorporated into a new application products group and their technology was integrated into Juniper's <a href="/wiki/Infranet" class="mw-redirect" title="Infranet">infranet</a> framework.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Afterwards, Juniper did not make any additional acquisitions until 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-vault_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vault-84">[84]</a></sup></p><p>From 2010 to September 2011, Juniper made six acquisitions and invested in eight companies. Often Juniper acquired early-stage startups, developing their technology, then selling it to pre-existing Juniper clients.<sup id="cite_ref-cross_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cross-59">[59]</a></sup> Juniper acquired two digital video companies, <a href="/wiki/Ankeena_Networks" title="Ankeena Networks">Ankeena Networks</a> and Blackwave Inc., as well as <a href="/wiki/Wireless_LAN" title="Wireless LAN">wireless LAN</a> software company <a href="/wiki/Trapeze_Networks" title="Trapeze Networks">Trapeze Networks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cross_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cross-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In 2012, Juniper acquired Mykonos Software, which develops security software intended to deceive hackers already within the network perimeter.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and a developer of software-defined network controllers, Contrail Systems.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In 2014 Juniper acquired the <a href="/wiki/Software-defined_networking" title="Software-defined networking">software-defined networking</a> (SDN) company WANDL.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2016, Juniper closed its acquisition of BTI, a provider of cloud and metro network technology, in an effort to beef up its data center interconnect and metro packet optical transport technology and services.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Juniper acquired cloud operations management and optimization startup AppFormix in December 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In 2017, Juniper bought Cyphort, a Silicon Valley startup that makes security analytics software.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Juniper acquired cloud storage company HTBASE in November 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In April 2019, Juniper acquired wireless LAN (WLAN) startup Mist Systems to bolster its software-defined enterprise portfolio and multicloud offerings.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Products" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<p>Juniper Networks designs and markets IT networking products, such as routers, switches and IT security products.<sup id="cite_ref-annualreport_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annualreport-94">[94]</a></sup> It started out selling core routers for ISPs, and expanded into edge routers, data centers, wireless networking, networking for branch offices and other access and aggregation devices.<sup id="cite_ref-annualreport_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annualreport-94">[94]</a></sup></p><p>Juniper is the third largest market-share holder overall for routers and switches used by ISPs.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> According to analyst firm Dell'Oro Group, it is the fourth largest for edge routers<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> and second for core routers with 25% of the core market.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> It is also the second largest market share holder for firewall products with a 24.8% share of the firewall market.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In data center <a href="/wiki/Security_appliance" title="Security appliance">security appliances</a>, Juniper is the second-place market-share holder behind Cisco.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In WLAN, where Juniper used to hold a more marginal market share, it is now expanding through its acquisition of Mist Systems, now a visionary in WLAN according to Gartner.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Juniper provides technical support and services through the J-Care program.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p><p>As of February 2020, Juniper's product families include the following:
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Routers_and_switches">Routers and switches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Routers and switches" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Juniper Networks' first product was the <a href="/wiki/Junos" class="mw-redirect" title="Junos">Junos</a> router operating system, which was released on July 1, 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The first Juniper router was made available that September and was a core router for internet service providers called the M40.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlichek2002_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlichek2002-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>8</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eleven_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-106">[106]</a></sup> It incorporated specialized <a href="/wiki/Application-specific_integrated_circuit" title="Application-specific integrated circuit">application-specific integrated circuits</a> (ASIC) for routing internet traffic that were developed in partnership with IBM.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> It had ten times the throughput of comparable contemporary Cisco products.<sup id="cite_ref-eleven_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The M40 was followed by the smaller M20 router in December 1999<sup id="cite_ref-one_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-one-9">[9]</a></sup> and the M160 in March 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p><p>By 2000, Juniper had developed five hardware systems and made seven new releases of its Junos operating system.<sup id="cite_ref-Inc2001_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inc2001-112">[112]</a></sup> That April, Juniper released the second generation of the internet processors embedded in its core routers.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In April 2002, Juniper released the first of the T-series family (originally known under the code-name Gibson), which could perform four times as many route lookups per second as the M160.<sup id="cite_ref-unveil_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unveil-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The first products of the TX Matrix family, which could be used to combine up to four T-series routers, was released in December 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p><p>By 2003, Juniper had diversified into three major router applications: core routers, edge routers and routers for mobile traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavlichek2002_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlichek2002-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>9</span></sup> Juniper's first major diversification from core routers was when it entered the market for edge routers, by acquiring the e-series product family (originally known as ERX) through the purchase of Unisphere in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-fifteen_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fifteen-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cautious_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cautious-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> By 2002, both Cisco and Juniper had increased their focus on edge routers, because many ISPs had built up abundant bandwidth at the core.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Several improvements to Juniper's software and its broadband aggregation features were released in late 2003. At this time, Juniper had the largest market-share (52%) of the broadband aggregation market.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In 2003, Juniper entered the market for cable-modem termination systems with the G-series product family after the acquisition of Pacific Broadband.<sup id="cite_ref-multichannel_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-multichannel-78">[78]</a></sup> The product family was discontinued later that year.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p><p>Juniper's first enterprise switch product was the EX 4200, which was released in 2008. In a comparative technical test, <i>Network World</i> said the EX4200 was the top performer out of network switches they tested in latency and throughput, but its multicast features were "newer and less robust" than other aspects of the product.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Juniper Networks announced the T1600 1.6 Terabits per second core router in 2007 and the newer T4000 4 Terabit router in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> In 2012 it released the ACX family of universal access routers.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In 2013, the company made several new releases in the MX family of edge routers: it introduced a smaller version of its core routers called PTX3000, and several new enterprise routers were released.<sup id="cite_ref-SWOT_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWOT-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Seven months later, Juniper acquired WANDL, and its technology was integrated into the NorthStar WAN controller Juniper announced in February 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-nwww_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nwww-128">[128]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2011, Juniper introduced QFabric, a proprietary protocol<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> methodology for transferring data over a network using a single network layer. Several individual products for the QFabric methodology were released throughout the year.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> In October 2013, Juniper introduced another network architecture called MetaFabric and a new set of switches, the QFX5100 family, as one of the foundations of the new architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-dafbdjna_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dafbdjna-131">[131]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2014, several software and hardware improvements were introduced for Juniper routers, including a series of software applications ISPs could use to provide internet-based services to consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-nwww_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nwww-128">[128]</a></sup> In December 2014, Juniper introduced a network switch, OCX1100, that could run on either the Junos operating system or the <a href="/wiki/Open_Compute_Project" title="Open Compute Project">Open Compute Project</a> open-source software.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Security">Security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Security" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Juniper_Networks_SRX5800_service_gateway_and_security_appliance.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Juniper_Networks_SRX5800_service_gateway_and_security_appliance.jpg/220px-Juniper_Networks_SRX5800_service_gateway_and_security_appliance.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="346" data-file-height="500"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 318px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Juniper_Networks_SRX5800_service_gateway_and_security_appliance.jpg/220px-Juniper_Networks_SRX5800_service_gateway_and_security_appliance.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="318" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Juniper_Networks_SRX5800_service_gateway_and_security_appliance.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juniper Networks SRX5800 service gateway and security appliance</div></div></div>
<p>Juniper Networks introduced the JProtect security toolkit in May 2003. It included firewalls, flow monitoring, filtering and <a href="/wiki/Network_Address_Translation" class="mw-redirect" title="Network Address Translation">Network Address Translation</a> (NAT).<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Through the 2004 acquisition of <a href="/wiki/NetScreen_Technologies" title="NetScreen Technologies">NetScreen Technologies</a>, Juniper acquired the Juniper Secure Meeting product line,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Remote_desktop_software" title="Remote desktop software">remote desktop access software</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The NetScreen-5GT ADSL security appliance was the first new NetScreen product Juniper introduced after the acquisition<sup id="cite_ref-twentyfour_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twentyfour-138">[138]</a></sup> and its first wireless product.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The first Juniper product intended for small businesses was a remote access appliance that was released in August 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> An open interface for the development of third party tools for the appliance was made available that September.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2004, Juniper entered the market for enterprise access routers with three routers that were the first of the J-series product family. It used the channel partners acquired with NetScreen to take the routers to market.<sup id="cite_ref-twentyfour_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twentyfour-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Juniper released its first dedicated <a href="/wiki/Network_Access_Control" title="Network Access Control">Network Access Control</a> (NAC) product in late 2005, which was followed by the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Funk_Software" title="Funk Software">Funk Software</a> for its NAC capabilities for switches.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> According to a 2006 review in <i>Network World</i>, Juniper's SSG 520 firewall and routing product was "the first serious threat" to competing products from Cisco.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Juniper released the SRX family of gateway products in 2008. The gateways sold well, but customers and resellers reported a wide range of technical issues starting in 2010, which Juniper did not acknowledge until 2012, when it began providing updates to the product software.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2011, Juniper and AT&amp;T announced they would jointly develop the AT&amp;T Mobile Security application based on Juniper's Pulse security software.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In May 2012, Juniper released a series of new features for the web security software it acquired from Mykonos Software that February. Mykonos' software is focused on deceiving hackers by presenting fake vulnerabilities and tracking their activity.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In January 2014, Juniper announced the Firefly Suite of security and switching products for <a href="/wiki/Virtual_machines" class="mw-redirect" title="Virtual machines">virtual machines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The following month Juniper Networks released several products for "intrusion deception", which create fake files, store incorrect passwords and change network maps in order to confuse hackers that have already penetrated the network perimeter.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Juniper_networks_backdoor_admin_password_hidden_in_code.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Juniper_networks_backdoor_admin_password_hidden_in_code.png/220px-Juniper_networks_backdoor_admin_password_hidden_in_code.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="59" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1469" data-file-height="391"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 59px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Juniper_networks_backdoor_admin_password_hidden_in_code.png/220px-Juniper_networks_backdoor_admin_password_hidden_in_code.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="59" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Juniper_networks_backdoor_admin_password_hidden_in_code.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marked in yellow: backdoor admin password hidden in the code</div></div></div>
<p>An analysis of Juniper's <a href="/wiki/ScreenOS" title="ScreenOS">ScreenOS</a> firmware code in December 2015 discovered a backdoor key using <a href="/wiki/Dual_EC_DRBG" title="Dual EC DRBG">Dual_EC_DRBG</a> allowing to passively decrypt the traffic encrypted by ScreenOS. This backdoor was inserted in the year 2008 into the versions of ScreenOS from 6.2.0r15 to 6.2.0r18 and from 6.3.0r12 to 6.3.0r20<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> and gives any user administrative access when using a special master password.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Some analysts claim that this backdoor still exists in ScreenOS.<sup id="cite_ref-wired-secret-code-in-junipers-firewalls_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired-secret-code-in-junipers-firewalls-153">[153]</a></sup> Stephen Checkoway was quoted in Wired that "If this backdoor was not intentional, then, in my opinion, it’s an amazing coincidence."<sup id="cite_ref-wired.com_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired.com-154">[154]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2015, Juniper Systems announced that they had discovered "unauthorized code" in the ScreenOS software that underlies their NetScreen devices, present from 2012 onwards. There were two vulnerabilities: One was a simple root password backdoor, and the other one was changing a point in Dual_EC_DRBG so that the attackers presumably had the key to use the preexisting (intentional or unintentional) <a href="/wiki/Kleptographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Kleptographic">kleptographic</a> backdoor in ScreenOS to passively decrypt traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_defined_networking">Software defined networking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Software defined networking" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>According to a 2014 <a href="/wiki/SWOT_analysis" title="SWOT analysis">SWOT analysis</a> by MarketLine, in recent history Juniper has been focusing on <a href="/wiki/Software-defined_networking" title="Software-defined networking">software-defined networking</a> (SDN).<sup id="cite_ref-SWOT_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWOT-126">[126]</a></sup> It acquired SDN company Contrail Systems in December 2012. The following month Juniper announced its SDN strategy, which included a new licensing model based on usage and new features for the Junos operating system.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> In February 2013, Juniper released several SDN products, including the application provisioning software, Services Activation Director and the Mobile Control Gateway appliance.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2013, Juniper announced an SDN controller called JunosV Contrail, using technology it acquired through Contrail Systems.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> A series of SDN products were released in February 2014, such as a network management software product, Junos Fusion, and an SDN controller called NorthStar. Northstar helps find the optimal path for data to travel through a network.<sup id="cite_ref-nwww_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nwww-128">[128]</a></sup></p><p>Every year, since 2009, Juniper holds SDN Throwdown competition to encourage students from universities across the world to access NorthStar Controller and build a solution around it to optimize network throughput. In 2019, team from Rutgers University led by PhD student, Sumit Maheshwari won this competition.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_updates">Recent updates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Recent updates" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In March 2015, Juniper announced a series of updates to the PTX family of core routers, the QFX family of switches, as well as updates to its security portfolio.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> According to a report published by technology consulting firm <a href="/wiki/LexInnova_Technologies" title="LexInnova Technologies">LexInnova</a>, as of June 2015 Juniper Networks was the third largest recipient of network security-related patents with portfolio of 2,926 security-related patents.<sup id="cite_ref-CSO_2015_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSO_2015-163">[163]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2018, Juniper announced a new offering called EngNet, which is a set of developer tools and information meant to help companies move toward automation, and replace the typical command-line interface.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Operations" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<p>Juniper Networks has operations in more than 100 countries. Around 50% of its revenue is from the United States, 30% is from <a href="/wiki/Europe,_the_Middle_East_and_Africa" title="Europe, the Middle East and Africa">EMEA</a> and 20% is from Asia. Juniper sells directly to businesses, as well as through resale and distribution partners, such as Ericsson, IBM, Nokia, IngramMicro and NEC.<sup id="cite_ref-vault_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vault-84">[84]</a></sup> About 50% of Juniper's revenues are derived from routers, 13% from switches, 12% comes from IT security and 25% from services.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p><p>According to a 2013 report by <a href="/wiki/Glassdoor" title="Glassdoor">Glassdoor</a>, Juniper Networks has the highest paid software engineers in the technology sector by a margin of about $24,000 per year.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> It operates the Juniper Networks Academic Alliance (JNAA) program, which scouts fresh college graduates.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p><p>According to a <a href="/wiki/SWOT_analysis" title="SWOT analysis">SWOT analysis</a> by <a href="/wiki/MarketLine" class="mw-redirect" title="MarketLine">MarketLine</a>, Juniper has "a strong focus" on research and development. R&amp;D expenses have been between 22 and 25% of revenue from 2011 to 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-SWOT_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWOT-126">[126]</a></sup> Most of the company's manufacturing is outsourced<sup id="cite_ref-twelve_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twelve-11">[11]</a></sup> to three manufacturing companies: <a href="/wiki/Celestica" title="Celestica">Celestica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flextronics" class="mw-redirect" title="Flextronics">Flextronics</a> and Accton Technology.<sup id="cite_ref-vault_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vault-84">[84]</a></sup> Juniper operates the Junos Innovation Fund, which was started with $50 million in 2010 and invests in early-stage technology companies developing applications for the Junos operating system.<sup id="cite_ref-cross_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cross-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nnwwodna_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nnwwodna-168">[168]</a></sup> As of 2011, Juniper Networks invested in 20 companies. This is estimated to be 1 to 2% of the companies it has evaluated for a potential investment.<sup id="cite_ref-cross_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cross-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: See also" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r936637989">.mw-parser-output .portal{border:solid #aaa 1px;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style><div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright">
<ul><li><span><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/28px-Industry5.svg.png" decoding="async" width="28" height="28" class="noviewer" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 28px;height: 28px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/28px-Industry5.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="28" data-height="28" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/42px-Industry5.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/56px-Industry5.svg.png 2x" data-class="noviewer"> </span></span><span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Companies" title="Portal:Companies">Companies portal</a></span></li></ul></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_networking_hardware_vendors" title="List of networking hardware vendors">List of networking hardware vendors</a></li></ul></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juniper_Networks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: References" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
